THE CORE HOOK & PREMISE (BOOK 1 ONLY)\nLogline: A grumpy West Texas rancher, drowning in a decade of self-imposed penance, is forced into close quarters with the sunny, city-returned veterinarian taking over his town's clinic—the one woman who sees the boy he used to be behind the armor of the man he’s forced himself to become.\nThe Inciting Incident: Dr. Ray \"Doc\" Delaney is retiring, and his daughter, Dr. Wren Delaney, returns from Dallas to take over the veterinary clinic. Because the clinic's apartment is unusable and her budget is shot, Maeve Holloway \"intervenes\" and rents Wren the Barn Apartment on the Lantern Ranch.\nThe Core Conflict: Wyatt has spent ten years acting exactly like his late father, Hank, as penance for their final fight. He doesn't want a bubbly, floral-wearing vet living on his ranch, reminding him of the vet school dream he gave up. Wren is fighting her own quiet burnout and needs this clinic to work, but she refuses to let Wyatt's grumpiness dim her chosen light.\nThe Stakes:\nProfessional: The health of the Lantern Ranch livestock depends on their working relationship.\nPersonal: If Wyatt can't let go of his guilt, he will lose the ranch to his own misery. If Wren can't prove herself to the stubborn locals (starting with Wyatt), her dream of taking over her father's legacy dies.\n\n2. THE TROPES & MARKETING ANCHORS\nPrimary Trope: Grumpy / Sunshine\nSecondary Trope: Forced Proximity (Landlord/Tenant, living on the same ranch)\nTertiary Trope: Childhood Friend's Older Brother / Small Town\nWyatt’s Arc: From Penance to Permission. Wyatt must realize that sacrificing his own happiness doesn't bring his father back. Wren gives him permission to stop being Hank, and finally be Wyatt.\nWren’s Arc: Chosen Joy. Wren must prove that her optimistic, colorful approach to life isn't naive—it is a hard-won shield against a dark past, and it is exactly what makes her a brilliant vet.\n\n3. MAIN CHARACTERS (BOOK 1 FOCUS)\nWYATT HOLLOWAY (34) – The Dutiful One\nRole: Protagonist / Hero\nPhysicality: 6'3\", broad, built from hard labor. Dark hair, dark eyes, perpetual two-day stubble. Calloused hands. Wears Wranglers, work shirts, and a weathered Stetson.\nInternal Wound: Fought with Hank the morning of the fire. Said, \"I hope I never turn into you.\" Hank died hours later. Wyatt has spent the last ten years trying to be Hank to make up for it, abandoning his dream of vet school to run the ranch.\nArmor: Silence, grumpiness, relentless work.\nWhat he thinks of Wren: She is too bright, too loud, wears too many florals, and represents the career path he had to choke out of himself. She is a distraction he can't afford.\nWhat he actually feels: She makes him want things he swore he gave up the right to have.\nWREN DELANEY, DVM (28) – The Defiant Sunshine\nRole: Protagonist / Heroine\nPhysicality: 5'5\", curvy-soft, honey-brown hair in a practical braid with flyaways. Freckles that pop in the Texas sun.\nAesthetic: \"Anthropologie catalog but make it manure-proof.\" Floral sundresses over jeans, embroidered Carhartt jacket, Etsy cowboy boots. Drives a beat-up Subaru Outback filled with dog hair and granola bars.\nInternal Wound: Lost a patient during a brutal emergency shift in Dallas, leading to a quiet emotional collapse. She is recovering from severe burnout and feelings of professional inadequacy.\nArmor: \"Chosen sunshine.\" Humming, bright colors, making excellent coffee. Her joy is an active, daily decision to fight the darkness.\nWhat she thinks of Wyatt: He’s a stubborn, closed-off wall of a man who is actively standing in his own way—and she remembers when he used to smile.\n\n4. KEY LOCATIONS FOR BOOK 1\nThe Lantern Ranch (The Main Setting):\nThe Barn Apartment: Wren’s home. Converted loft above the working barn, accessed by an exterior staircase. Cozy, small, features a bathtub under a window overlooking the pastures.\nThe Main House: Where Maeve lives. Wraparound porch, kitchen always smelling of coffee and pie. Wyatt and the brothers gather here for Sunday Pot Roast.\nWyatt’s House: A small, spare, deliberately lonely house a quarter-mile from the main house.\nThe Delaney Veterinary Clinic: Located on Main Street, two doors down from the café. It still has Doc’s name on the door (Wren’s goal is to finally paint her own name there).\nThe Lantern Café: Maeve’s diner on Main Street. The town's heartbeat. Red vinyl booths, prominent pie case.\nThe Branding Iron: The local bar where Rhett bartends. Wyatt occasionally goes here to brood; Wren goes here to prove she can hang with the locals.\nDoc Delaney's House: Blue clapboard house with the porch swing—the ultimate setting for quiet, emotional confessions.\n\n5. SUBPLOT & SERIES SEEDS (WHAT TO PLANT IN BOOK 1)\nAs you write Book 1, you must lay the groundwork for the overarching series mystery (The Fire) and the future books, but only through Wyatt and Wren's eyes.\nThe Mystery Seeds to Plant in Book 1:\nThe Unlit Lantern: Introduce the cast-iron carriage lantern hanging unlit on the ranch gate. Establish that it hasn't been lit since the fire. Wyatt should look at it with guilt; Wren should notice its silence.\nWyatt's Secret: Wyatt must confess to Wren (likely on Doc's porch swing or in the Barn Apartment) the fight he had with his father the morning of the fire. This is his emotional climax.\nJesse’s Behavior: Jesse (the youngest) must be portrayed as quiet and hyper-focused on the horses.\nCrucial Seed: During a small test-run rodeo or livestock event, someone lights a cigarette. Show Jesse having a subtle, physical reaction of panic or avoidance. Wyatt notices but chalks it up to general grief.\nMaeve’s Watchfulness: Show Maeve watching Jesse leave the Sunday dinner table. Let the reader see her quiet, knowing sadness.\nFuture Book Setups:\nCaleb (Book 2 Setup): Caleb is off-page for 99% of the book, but his absence must be felt. Wyatt feels abandoned by him.\nThe Cliffhanger/Ending of Book 1: In the final scene of Book 1, after Wyatt and Wren have found their happy ending, headlights sweep across the Lantern Ranch driveway. An engine cuts. A car door opens. Caleb is home.\nSamuel (Book 3 Setup): Samuel should attend Sunday Pot Roast. He says almost nothing, but his intense, brooding presence and his military background should be established.\nRhett (Book 4 Setup): Rhett should be the easy-going, teasing brother who immediately champions Wren and makes fun of Wyatt's grumpy pining. Establish his charm at the Branding Iron.\n\n6. MAEVE’S PIE LEXICON (BOOK 1 CHEAT SHEET)\nUse these specific pies in Book 1 to signal Maeve’s emotional states to the reader:\nApple Pie: (Pleased / All is well) Maeve serves this when Wren first moves into the Barn Apartment.\nPecan Pie: (Grief / Unspoken sorrow) Maeve bakes this on the anniversary of the fire, or when Wyatt is particularly closed off.\nKey Lime Pie: (She knows something you don't) Maeve slides a slice of this to Wyatt when she notices him staring at Wren’s apartment window.\nPeach Pie: (Welcome home) Maeve bakes this for Wren when she officially handles her first major veterinary emergency in town.\n\n7. WRITING RULES FOR BOOK 1\nShow, Don't Tell Wyatt's Vet Dream: Don't just say Wyatt wanted to be a vet. Show him assisting Wren with a difficult calving. Show his hands—normally rough—becoming incredibly gentle and precise. Let Wren recognize his natural, wasted talent.\nKeep the Pace Tight: Keep the focus on the ranch and the clinic. The tension should build from their proximity—Wren walking down her stairs in the morning, Wyatt pretending not to watch her from the stables.\nThe Sunday Dinner Rule: You must have at least one Sunday Pot Roast scene. Use this to show the chaotic, masculine dynamic of the brothers, Maeve's quiet matriarchal power, and how Wren fits (or struggles to fit) into the family dynamic.\nNo \"Magic\" Healing: Wyatt’s grief and guilt are ten years deep. Wren shouldn't \"cure\" him in a single conversation. His healing must be earned through actions—specifically, him letting go of the reins of the ranch long enough to let himself love her.\n\nDo not include any pleasantries, just the synopsis text."
  }

When his father Hank died in a ranch fire hours after their worst fight—the morning Wyatt told him he'd never want to be like him—Wyatt swallowed his dream of veterinary school whole and became Hank instead, rough edges and all, as if sheer penance could settle a debt with a dead man. He runs the Lantern Ranch with his jaw set and his heart locked, and the unlit carriage lantern hanging at the gate suits him fine. He doesn't deserve the light. Then Dr. Wren Delaney comes home to Mariposa, Texas, floral sundresses and granola bars and all, to take over her father's veterinary clinic—and Maeve Holloway, the family's quietly omniscient matriarch, rents her the Barn Apartment on the Lantern Ranch before Wyatt can form a single objection. Suddenly the one woman who represents every dream Wyatt buried is living forty yards from his bedroom window, humming in the morning dark and smelling like coffee and wildflowers, and the armor he's spent a decade welding shut starts to crack at every seam.\n\nWren Delaney knows exactly what she looks like to men like Wyatt: naive, soft, too much color for a serious world. She has let people believe that her relentless brightness is simply who she is, because the truth—that she built her sunshine like a fortress, brick by deliberate brick, after a brutal Dallas emergency shift gutted her professional confidence and left her quietly falling apart—is hers to carry alone. Coming home to Mariposa isn't retreat; it's reclamation. She intends to earn her name on that clinic door and prove to every stubborn rancher in the county that she is exactly as capable as her father. What she doesn't expect is Wyatt—surly and immovable and heartbreaking in his self-imposed exile—or the way her memory of the boy he used to be keeps surfacing when she watches his calloused hands turn impossibly gentle during a difficult calving, revealing the gifted, wasted talent underneath all that deliberate hardness. She refuses to fix him. She refuses to dim herself for him. But she cannot stop herself from wanting him to choose to come back to life.\n\nTheir proximity does its slow, inevitable work: livestock emergencies that require trust, Sunday dinners at Maeve's table where the brothers orbit Wren like she's always belonged, and late-night conversations on the Barn Apartment steps where ten years of silence begin to fracture. When Wyatt finally tells Wren about the fight—the exact words he said to his father the morning of the fire—it costs him everything he has left to give, and it is the first honest thing he has offered the world in a decade. Their hard-won beginning is earned not through grand gestures but through the small, daily rebellions of a man learning to want his own life again and a woman who has always known that joy is not a gift but a choice. The night they finally find their footing, headlights sweep slow across the Lantern Ranch drive. An engine cuts. A car door opens in the dark. Caleb Holloway, absent for years and carrying something the family doesn't yet understand, has come home—and the questions surrounding the fire that broke this family are only beginning to surface.\n\nFormat your response EXACTLY as a JSON array of objects with keys: \"name\", \"role\", and \"description\". Do not include Markdown blocks like ```json, just return the raw array."

Suddenly the one woman who represents every dream Wyatt buried is living forty yards from his bedroom window, humming in the morning dark and smelling like coffee and wildflowers, and the armor he's spent a decade welding shut starts to crack at every seam.\n\nWren Delaney knows exactly what she looks like to men like Wyatt: naive, soft, too much color for a serious world. She has let people believe that her relentless brightness is simply who she is, because the truth—that she built her sunshine like a fortress, brick by deliberate brick, after a brutal Dallas emergency shift gutted her professional confidence and left her quietly falling apart—is hers to carry alone. Coming home to Mariposa isn't retreat; it's reclamation. She intends to earn her name on that clinic door and prove to every stubborn rancher in the county that she is exactly as capable as her father. What she doesn't expect is Wyatt—surly and immovable and heartbreaking in his self-imposed exile—or the way her memory of the boy he used to be keeps surfacing when she watches his calloused hands turn impossibly gentle during a difficult calving, revealing the gifted, wasted talent underneath all that deliberate hardness. She refuses to fix him. She refuses to dim herself for him. But she cannot stop herself from wanting him to choose to come back to life.\n\nTheir proximity does its slow, inevitable work: livestock emergencies that require trust, Sunday dinners at Maeve's table where the brothers orbit Wren like she's always belonged, and late-night conversations on the Barn Apartment steps where ten years of silence begin to fracture. When Wyatt finally tells Wren about the fight—the exact words he said to his father the morning of the fire—it costs him everything he has left to give, and it is the first honest thing he has offered the world in a decade. Their hard-won beginning is earned not through grand gestures but through the small, daily rebellions of a man learning to want his own life again and a woman who has always known that joy is not a gift but a choice. The night they finally find their footing, headlights sweep slow across the Lantern Ranch drive. An engine cuts. A car door opens in the dark. Caleb Holloway, absent for years and carrying something the family doesn't yet understand, has come home—and the questions surrounding the fire that broke this family are only beginning to surface.\nCharacters: [{\"id\":\"f40368b8-4974-4dce-ae9c-24ccc3badd97\",\"name\":\"Wyatt Holloway\",\"role\":\"Male Lead / Hero\",\"description\":\"The eldest Holloway son and owner of Lantern Ranch, Wyatt has spent ten years punishing himself for the last words he said to his father before a fatal fire. He dismantled his dream of becoming a veterinarian and rebuilt himself in his father's rough image, treating duty as penance. THE CORE HOOK & PREMISE (BOOK 1 ONLY)\nLogline: A grumpy West Texas rancher, drowning in a decade of self-imposed penance, is forced into close quarters with the sunny, city-returned veterinarian taking over his town's clinic—the one woman who sees the boy he used to be behind the armor of the man he’s forced himself to become.\nThe Inciting Incident: Dr. Ray \"Doc\" Delaney is retiring, and his daughter, Dr. Wren Delaney, returns from Dallas to take over the veterinary clinic. Because the clinic's apartment is unusable and her budget is shot, Maeve Holloway \"intervenes\" and rents Wren the Barn Apartment on the Lantern Ranch.\nThe Core Conflict: Wyatt has spent ten years acting exactly like his late father, Hank, as penance for their final fight. Wren gives him permission to stop being Hank, and finally be Wyatt.\nWren’s Arc: Chosen Joy. Wren must prove that her optimistic, colorful approach to life isn't naive—it is a hard-won shield against a dark past, and it is exactly what makes her a brilliant vet.\n\n3. When his father Hank died in a ranch fire hours after their worst fight—the morning Wyatt told him he'd never want to be like him—Wyatt swallowed his dream of veterinary school whole and became Hank instead, rough edges and all, as if sheer penance could settle a debt with a dead man. He runs the Lantern Ranch with his jaw set and his heart locked, and the unlit carriage lantern hanging at the gate suits him fine. He doesn't deserve the light. Then Dr. Wren Delaney comes home to Mariposa, Texas, floral sundresses and granola bars and all, to take over her father's veterinary clinic—and Maeve Holloway, the family's quietly omniscient matriarch, rents her the Barn Apartment on the Lantern Ranch before Wyatt can form a single objection. Suddenly the one woman who represents every dream Wyatt buried is living forty yards from his bedroom window, humming in the morning dark and smelling like coffee and wildflowers, and the armor he's spent a decade welding shut starts to crack at every seam.\n\nWren Delaney knows exactly what she looks like to men like Wyatt: naive, soft, too much color for a serious world. She has let people believe that her relentless brightness is simply who she is, because the truth—that she built her sunshine like a fortress, brick by deliberate brick, after a brutal Dallas emergency shift gutted her professional confidence and left her quietly falling apart—is hers to carry alone. Coming home to Mariposa isn't retreat; it's reclamation. She intends to earn her name on that clinic door and prove to every stubborn rancher in the county that she is exactly as capable as her father. What she doesn't expect is Wyatt—surly and immovable and heartbreaking in his self-imposed exile—or the way her memory of the boy he used to be keeps surfacing when she watches his calloused hands turn impossibly gentle during a difficult calving, revealing the gifted, wasted talent underneath all that deliberate hardness. She refuses to fix him. She refuses to dim herself for him. But she cannot stop herself from wanting him to choose to come back to life.\n\nTheir proximity does its slow, inevitable work: livestock emergencies that require trust, Sunday dinners at Maeve's table where the brothers orbit Wren like she's always belonged, and late-night conversations on the Barn Apartment steps where ten years of silence begin to fracture. When Wyatt finally tells Wren about the fight—the exact words he said to his father the morning of the fire—it costs him everything he has left to give, and it is the first honest thing he has offered the world in a decade. Their hard-won beginning is earned not through grand gestures but through the small, daily rebellions of a man learning to want his own life again and a woman who has always known that joy is not a gift but a choice. The night they finally find their footing, headlights sweep slow across the Lantern Ranch drive. An engine cuts. A car door opens in the dark. THE CORE HOOK & PREMISE (BOOK 1 ONLY)\nLogline: A grumpy West Texas rancher, drowning in a decade of self-imposed penance, is forced into close quarters with the sunny, city-returned veterinarian taking over his town's clinic—the one woman who sees the boy he used to be behind the armor of the man he’s forced himself to become.\nThe Inciting Incident: Dr. Ray \"Doc\" Delaney is retiring, and his daughter, Dr. Wren Delaney, returns from Dallas to take over the veterinary clinic. Because the clinic's apartment is unusable and her budget is shot, Maeve Holloway \"intervenes\" and rents Wren the Barn Apartment on the Lantern Ranch.\nThe Core Conflict: Wyatt has spent ten years acting exactly like his late father, Hank, as penance for their final fight. He doesn't want a bubbly, floral-wearing vet living on his ranch, reminding him of the vet school dream he gave up. Wren gives him permission to stop being Hank, and finally be Wyatt.\nWren’s Arc: Chosen Joy. Wren must prove that her optimistic, colorful approach to life isn't naive—it is a hard-won shield against a dark past, and it is exactly what makes her a brilliant vet.\n\n3. When his father Hank died in a ranch fire hours after their worst fight—the morning Wyatt told him he'd never want to be like him—Wyatt swallowed his dream of veterinary school whole and became Hank instead, rough edges and all, as if sheer penance could settle a debt with a dead man. He runs the Lantern Ranch with his jaw set and his heart locked, and the unlit carriage lantern hanging at the gate suits him fine. He doesn't deserve the light. She has let people believe that her relentless brightness is simply who she is, because the truth—that she built her sunshine like a fortress, brick by deliberate brick, after a brutal Dallas emergency shift gutted her professional confidence and left her quietly falling apart—is hers to carry alone. She has let people believe that her relentless brightness is simply who she is, because the truth—that she built her sunshine like a fortress, brick by deliberate brick, after a brutal Dallas emergency shift gutted her professional confidence and left her quietly falling apart—is hers to carry alone. Coming home to Mariposa isn't retreat; it's reclamation. She intends to earn her name on that clinic door and prove to every stubborn rancher in the county that she is exactly as capable as her father. What she doesn't expect is Wyatt—surly and immovable and heartbreaking in his self-imposed exile—or the way her memory of the boy he used to be keeps surfacing when she watches his calloused hands turn impossibly gentle during a difficult calving, revealing the gifted, wasted talent underneath all that deliberate hardness. She refuses to fix him. She refuses to dim herself for him. But she cannot stop herself from wanting him to choose to come back to life.\n\nTheir proximity does its slow, inevitable work: livestock emergencies that require trust, Sunday dinners at Maeve's table where the brothers orbit Wren like she's always belonged, and late-night conversations on the Barn Apartment steps where ten years of silence begin to fracture. When Wyatt finally tells Wren about the fight—the exact words he said to his father the morning of the fire—it costs him everything he has left to give, and it is the first honest thing he has offered the world in a decade. Their hard-won beginning is earned not through grand gestures but through the small, daily rebellions of a man learning to want his own life again and a woman who has always known that joy is not a gift but a choice. The night they finally find their footing, headlights sweep slow across the Lantern Ranch drive. An engine cuts. A car door opens in the dark. When she steps out, she is a vibrant, floral-wearing intrusion on his gray world. Wyatt is furious that Maeve rented out the Barn Apartment without his permission. The confrontation is immediate, tense, and charged with old history (she is his younger brother's childhood friend; she remembers when he used to smile).\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Wyatt is instantly triggered. Wren represents the veterinary career he forced himself to abandon to become his father. He must keep her at a distance to keep his guilt intact.\nChapter 3: No Way 1\nPOV: Wren\nRTB Beat: No Way 1 (The initial rejection of the romance)\nThe Plot: Wren moves her things into the cozy Barn Apartment. She tries to establish a neighborly truce by bringing Wyatt coffee, but he completely shuts her down, acting as harsh and withholding as his father, Hank, used to be. Wren realizes that living forty yards from this grumpy, closed-off wall of a man is going to be a battle.\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Wren’s \"chosen sunshine\" is put to the test. She resolves not to let his misery dim her light, while Wyatt reinforces his emotional armor.\nChapter 4: The Adhesion\nPOV: Wyatt\nRTB Beat: The Adhesion (The forces pulling them into the same orbit)\nThe Plot: A late-night emergency occurs on the ranch—a heifer is having a highly dangerous, breach calving. Wren is called out in the dark. Because she's short-staffed, Wyatt is forced to assist her. During the intense, muddy, high-stakes procedure, Wyatt's rough hands become incredibly gentle and precise. Wren recognizes his natural, wasted veterinary talent. They save the calf together.\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Show, Don't Tell Wyatt's Vet Dream. Working together, their bodies in close contact, they experience a powerful moment of mutual respect and physical awareness. Wyatt realizes he cannot ignore her presence.\nPHASE 2: THE FALL (Chapters 5 - 8)\nGoal: Deepen the connection, challenge their individual lies, and build the romantic tension.\n\nChapter 5: No Way 2\nPOV: Wren\nRTB Beat: No Way 2 (The external/internal obstacles rise)\nThe Plot: Wren faces her first major day of skepticism from the local, old-school ranchers at the clinic, who doubt a \"city girl\" can handle the job. She is exhausted. To make matters worse, Wyatt is actively avoiding her on the ranch, reeling from the intimacy of the calving night. He tries to convince himself that she is a distraction he can't afford.\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Wren is fighting professional inadequacy; Wyatt is fighting the terrifying realization that she makes him want things again.\nChapter 6: The Midpoint (Inkling of Desire)\nPOV: Wyatt\nRTB Beat: The Midpoint / Inkling of Desire (The turning point of attraction)\nThe Plot: The Sunday Dinner. Wren is invited to Sunday Pot Roast at the Main House. We see the chaotic, masculine dynamic of the brothers (Garrett teasing, Jesse quiet). Maeve slides Wyatt a slice of Key Lime Pie (She knows something you don't) because she caught him staring at the Barn Apartment window. Later that night, Wyatt finds Wren sitting on her exterior stairs, overwhelmed by the pressure of the clinic.\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Wyatt sits with her in the dark. The physical tension peaks, and they share a breathless, emotionally charged near-miss or a highly charged first kiss.\nChapter 7: Deepening the Connection\nPOV: Wren\nRTB Beat: Deepening of Proximity / Vulnerability\nThe Plot: The day after their moment on the stairs. Wren’s defenses are down. While treating a horse at the ranch, she quietly confesses to Wyatt about the brutal emergency shift in Dallas that broke her confidence. Instead of judging her, Wyatt’s quiet, steady presence offers her a safe harbor she hasn't had in a year.\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Wren realizes her sunshine doesn't have to be a fortress when she's around him. She is letting him see her in the dark.\nChapter 8: Inkling of Love\nPOV: Wyatt\nRTB Beat: The Inkling of Love (Recognizing the depth of feeling)\nThe Plot: Wyatt steps up to defend Wren's professional honor. When a stubborn local rancher refuses to pay Wren or trust her diagnosis, Wyatt steps in, using his formidable reputation in the county to back her up. Later, Maeve bakes a Peach Pie (Welcome home) for Wren to celebrate her officially handling her first major solo clinic emergency.\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Wyatt realizes he doesn't just want her; he respects her, admires her, and is actively falling in love with her. But this realization terrifies him because it threatens his decade of self-imposed penance.\nPHASE 3: THE RETREAT (Chapters 9 - 12)\nGoal: The emotional stakes clash with the wounds, leading to the crisis and the \"Dark Night of the Soul.\"\n\nChapter 9: Shields Up\nPOV: Wren\nRTB Beat: The Retreat / Shields Up (The panic sets in)\nThe Plot: Wren senses Wyatt pulling back. The closer they get, the more Wyatt retreats into his harsh \"Hank\" persona, terrified that loving her is a betrayal of his dead father. Wren, dealing with the fragile state of her own recovery, realizes she cannot force him to choose life. She puts her own emotional walls back up to protect her heart.\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Both characters retreat to their default armor—Wren masks her hurt with professional distance; Wyatt hides behind relentless, punishing physical labor.\nChapter 10: The Crisis (The Fight)\nPOV: Wyatt\nRTB Beat: The Crisis (The emotional climax/argument)\nThe Plot: It is the anniversary of the fire. Maeve has baked a Pecan Pie (Grief/Unspoken sorrow). The tension on the ranch is suffocating. Wyatt is at his absolute darkest. When Wren tries to gently check on him, Wyatt snaps. In a brutal, heartbreaking argument, he tells her she is a distraction, that he never wanted her here, and that some people don't get to have \"happy endings.\"\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Wyatt weaponizes his stoicism to push her away completely, believing he is saving her from his own cursed life. Wren is devastated, her worst fears of not being \"enough\" realized.\nSeries Seed: During this tense day, a small livestock event occurs where someone lights a cigarette, and Jesse has a subtle, physical panic reaction. Wyatt notices but is too consumed by his own grief to process it.\nChapter 11: The Wasteland\nPOV: Wren\nRTB Beat: The Wasteland (The separation)\nThe Plot: Complete radio silence between the Barn Apartment and Wyatt’s house. Wren throws herself entirely into the clinic, finally prepping to paint her own name on the door, but her \"chosen joy\" feels hollow. She plans to look for another place to live as soon as her lease is up.\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Wren is grieving the loss of the boy Wyatt used to be and the man he could have been, realizing she cannot fix a man who insists on staying in a prison of his own making.\nChapter 12: The Dark Night of the Soul\nPOV: Wyatt\nRTB Beat: The Dark Night of the Soul\nThe Plot: Wyatt is alone in his spare, lonely house. He looks out the window at the dark Barn Apartment. Maeve confronts him, refusing to let him hide behind his father's ghost anymore. Wyatt realizes that acting like Hank hasn't brought his father back, hasn't saved the ranch, and has only succeeded in torturing the people who love him.\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Wyatt experiences the death of his Lie. He realizes that his penance is actually a form of cowardice, and that he is about to lose the only light he has ever known.\nPHASE 4: THE REKINDLE & HEA (Chapters 13 - 15)\nGoal: The breakthrough, the grand gesture, the emotional healing, and the setup for the next book.\n\nChapter 13: The Wake-Up Call\nPOV: Wren\nRTB Beat: The Wake-Up Call (The catalyst for the resolution)\nThe Plot: A sudden, severe storm hits Mariposa, threatening the ranch's livestock. Wren and Wyatt are forced to work side-by-side in the freezing rain to save the herd. Despite the emotional pain between them, their physical and professional synchronicity is flawless.\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: As the storm clears, the lingering anger washes away, leaving only raw, exposed truth. The stage is set for the ultimate confession.\nChapter 14: The Grand Gesture\nPOV: Wyatt\nRTB Beat: The Grand Gesture (The emotional breakthrough)\nThe Plot: Wyatt takes Wren to Doc Delaney's house. Sitting on the porch swing, Wyatt finally breaks his ten-year silence. He confesses the exact words he said to his father the morning of the fire: \"I hope I never turn into you.\" He weeps, letting go of the guilt. Wren holds him, offering him the \"permission\" he has denied himself for a decade.\nThe Emotional Undercurrent: Wyatt stops being Hank and finally chooses to be Wyatt. He walks to the ranch gate and, for the first time in ten years, lights the carriage lantern.\nChapter 15: Whole New World & The Hook\nPOV: Dual (Wren / Wyatt Epilogue)\nRTB Beat: Whole New World (The HEA) & The Cliffhanger\nThe Plot:\nPart 1 (Wren): Wren's name is officially painted on the clinic door. She has found her footing, her confidence, and her home.\nPart 2 (Wyatt): A sunny Sunday afternoon at the ranch. Wyatt is smiling, helping Wren pack her things to move from the Barn Apartment—not away from the ranch, but into his house. They are happy, healed, and looking toward the future.\nThe Cliffhanger: As dusk falls on the Lantern Ranch, the newly lit carriage lantern glows at the gate. Headlights sweep slow across the driveway. An engine cuts. A car door opens in the dark. Caleb Holloway is home.\nWriter's Note on Novella Pacing:\nWhen you begin outlining the specific scenes within this template, keep the sensory details high (the smell of rain on dry Texas dirt, the warmth of the coffee, the physical weight of Wyatt's Stetson). The voice is warm, atmospheric, and anchored in a deeply intimate deep POV. It delivers the sweeping, dust-scoured reality of West Texas through quiet, tension-filled dialogue, sensory-rich descriptions of the land, and visceral physical chemistry. The prose avoids melodrama, purple prose, and modern slang, opting instead for a timeless, rugged, and emotionally honest style.\nFull Voice DNA\nSentence Rhythm: Cadenced and natural. Sentences mimic the slow, deliberate drawl of West Texas—long, flowing compound clauses that capture the vast landscape, interrupted by short, blunt physical truths when the emotional stakes or sexual tension spikes.\nVocabulary Register: Level 2 (Accessible, concrete, and grounded). The language is deeply rooted in ranching life, local flora/fauna, and physical labor. It avoids overly academic words, high-tech jargon, and overly flowery metaphors.\nNarrative Distance: Close, intimate first-person (or tight third-person) deep POV. The reader should feel the heat of the summer sun on the back of their neck, the smell of sweet hay and rain, and the physical ache of unspoken longing.\nDialogue Style: Understated, quiet, and loaded with subtext. Cowboy characters speak with a dry, buttoned-up West Texas economy of words—love and pain are shown through actions, teasing, and small gestures rather than grand speeches. Female leads are sharp, emotionally articulate, and quick-witted. No character echoes another.\nPunctuation Fingerprint: Clean, traditional, and rhythmic. Uses em dashes sparingly only for sudden, jarring physical interruptions. Avoids ellipses for trailing thoughts, random colons, excessive italics, and bolding.\nInternal Monologue Style: Visceral and reflective. Thoughts are heavily tied to physical sensations (a tightening throat, a racing pulse, a calloused hand on skin) and the immediate environment. Avoids overly dramatic rhetorical questions or self-pitying spirals.\nImagery Patterns: Grounded, earthy, and sensory. Focuses on the elements: wind, dust, the weight of denim, the scent of cedarwood, leather, and rain. Avoids abstract or illogical comparisons (e.g., \"her soul was a storm\").\nPacing Signature: Slow-burn tension that builds to explosive, high-heat payoffs. Avoids the \"rule of three\" for list-making adjectives or verbs."

Keep it physical and raw.\nDo not group things in threes. Avoid lists of three adjectives, three verbs, or three short sentences in a row.\nDo not use interpretive commentary to explain a character's action (e.g., \"He looked away, showing his guilt\"). Let the action speak for itself.\nDo not write characters who apologize constantly or over-communicate their feelings early on. The tension relies on their silence and emotional armor.\nDo not use pointless bookend summary sentences at the end of a scene or chapter.\n\nVoice Markers\nShowing Emotion Physically\nInstead of: I was incredibly nervous and felt so small standing in front of him.\nWrite: My throat went dry as I stepped closer, my boots sinking into the gravel. I had to tilt my chin up just to meet his eyes.\nGrounded, Concrete Imagery\nInstead of: He smelled like a rugged fantasy of pine and dark secrets.\nWrite: He smelled of cedarwood, horse sweat, and the sharp, clean scent of the rain that had just started to hit the dirt.\nAvoiding the Rule of Three\nInstead of: He stood up, walked to the window, and stared out at the rain.\nWrite: He stood up and walked to the window, his eyes locking on the dark clouds rolling over the ridge.\nEliminating Interpretive Commentary\nInstead of: Maeve slid the plate toward me in a motherly gesture of peace.\nWrite: Maeve slid the warm plate of peach pie across the laminate counter, her eyes holding mine until I took the fork.\n\nBANNED WORDS (DO NOT USE THESE WORDS/PHRASES):\nAVOID these overused AI words and phrases: \"tapestry\", \"testament\", \"symphony\", \"labyrinth\", \"shivers down spine\", \"let out a breath they didn't know they were holding\", \"eyes flashed\", \"needless to say\", \"in a world where\", \"a dance of\", \"delve\".\n\nANTI-PATTERNS (AVOID THESE STRUCTURES):\nAVOID structural AI patterns:\n- Do not end chapters with moralizing summaries, rhetorical questions, or neat wrap-ups.\n- Avoid overly balanced dialogue where everyone speaks in complete, polite paragraphs.\n- Avoid sudden, unearned emotional shifts or overly therapeutic language (\"I see you\", \"your feelings are valid\").\n\n---------------------------------------\n\nWrite Chapter 1: \"The Lantern at the Gate\".\n\nChapter Summary: Wren Delaney drives her battered Subaru into Mariposa, Texas, the late-afternoon sun turning the scrubland gold and merciless. She is humming to herself—a habit she has never fully controlled—and her floral sundress and turquoise Etsy boots are a deliberate act of will against the gray exhaustion she is carrying underneath. She stops first at Delaney Veterinary Clinic, where her father's name is still stenciled on the frosted glass door in faded gold letters, and the living quarters behind the office turn out to be a disaster of collapsed shelving and a slow roof leak her father forgot to mention. Her professional armor holds, but just barely. Then Maeve Holloway appears, as if summoned, with an offer already decided: the Barn Apartment on the Lantern Ranch, clean and ready and only forty yards from the main house. Maeve serves Wren a slice of apple pie at the clinic's break table while the paperwork is settled, and the warmth of it—the deliberate, unhurried kindness—nearly cracks Wren open. Driving through the Lantern Ranch gate at dusk, Wren notices the unlit carriage lantern hanging dark at the post, its glass clouded with years of dust, and something about it lodges in her chest like a splinter she can't locate.\n\nOverall Novel Synopsis: In the sun-scorched heart of West Texas, Wyatt Holloway has spent ten years building a prison out of duty. When his father Hank died in a ranch fire hours after their worst fight—the morning Wyatt told him he'd never want to be like him—Wyatt swallowed his dream of veterinary school whole and became Hank instead, rough edges and all, as if sheer penance could settle a debt with a dead man. He runs the Lantern Ranch with his jaw set and his heart locked, and the unlit carriage lantern hanging at the gate suits him fine. He doesn't deserve the light. Then Dr. Wren Delaney comes home to Mariposa, Texas, floral sundresses and granola bars and all, to take over her father's veterinary clinic—and Maeve Holloway, the family's quietly omniscient matriarch, rents her the Barn Apartment on the Lantern Ranch before Wyatt can form a single objection. Suddenly the one woman who represents every dream Wyatt buried is living forty yards from his bedroom window, humming in the morning dark and smelling like coffee and wildflowers, and the armor he's spent a decade welding shut starts to crack at every seam.\n\nWren Delaney knows exactly what she looks like to men like Wyatt: naive, soft, too much color for a serious world. She has let people believe that her relentless brightness is simply who she is, because the truth—that she built her sunshine like a fortress, brick by deliberate brick, after a brutal Dallas emergency shift gutted her professional confidence and left her quietly falling apart—is hers to carry alone. Coming home to Mariposa isn't retreat; it's reclamation. She intends to earn her name on that clinic door and prove to every stubborn rancher in the county that she is exactly as capable as her father. What she doesn't expect is Wyatt—surly and immovable and heartbreaking in his self-imposed exile—or the way her memory of the boy he used to be keeps surfacing when she watches his calloused hands turn impossibly gentle during a difficult calving, revealing the gifted, wasted talent underneath all that deliberate hardness. She refuses to fix him. She refuses to dim herself for him. But she cannot stop herself from wanting him to choose to come back to life.\n\nTheir proximity does its slow, inevitable work: livestock emergencies that require trust, Sunday dinners at Maeve's table where the brothers orbit Wren like she's always belonged, and late-night conversations on the Barn Apartment steps where ten years of silence begin to fracture. When Wyatt finally tells Wren about the fight—the exact words he said to his father the morning of the fire—it costs him everything he has left to give, and it is the first honest thing he has offered the world in a decade. Their hard-won beginning is earned not through grand gestures but through the small, daily rebellions of a man learning to want his own life again and a woman who has always known that joy is not a gift but a choice. The night they finally find their footing, headlights sweep slow across the Lantern Ranch drive. An engine cuts. A car door opens in the dark. Caleb Holloway, absent for years and carrying something the family doesn't yet understand, has come home—and the questions surrounding the fire that broke this family are only beginning to surface.\n\nEnsure the chapter is well-written, engaging, and flows naturally. Do not include any out-of-character AI pleasantries. Begin the text immediately."

It delivers the sweeping, dust-scoured reality of West Texas through quiet, tension-filled dialogue, sensory-rich descriptions of the land, and visceral physical chemistry. The prose avoids melodrama, purple prose, and modern slang, opting instead for a timeless, rugged, and emotionally honest style.\nFull Voice DNA\nSentence Rhythm: Cadenced and natural. Sentences mimic the slow, deliberate drawl of West Texas—long, flowing compound clauses that capture the vast landscape, interrupted by short, blunt physical truths when the emotional stakes or sexual tension spikes.\nVocabulary Register: Level 2 (Accessible, concrete, and grounded). The language is deeply rooted in ranching life, local flora/fauna, and physical labor. It avoids overly academic words, high-tech jargon, and overly flowery metaphors.\nNarrative Distance: Close, intimate first-person (or tight third-person) deep POV. The reader should feel the heat of the summer sun on the back of their neck, the smell of sweet hay and rain, and the physical ache of unspoken longing.\nDialogue Style: Understated, quiet, and loaded with subtext. Cowboy characters speak with a dry, buttoned-up West Texas economy of words—love and pain are shown through actions, teasing, and small gestures rather than grand speeches. Female leads are sharp, emotionally articulate, and quick-witted. No character echoes another.\nPunctuation Fingerprint: Clean, traditional, and rhythmic. Uses em dashes sparingly only for sudden, jarring physical interruptions. Avoids ellipses for trailing thoughts, random colons, excessive italics, and bolding.\nInternal Monologue Style: Visceral and reflective. Thoughts are heavily tied to physical sensations (a tightening throat, a racing pulse, a calloused hand on skin) and the immediate environment. Avoids overly dramatic rhetorical questions or self-pitying spirals.\nImagery Patterns: Grounded, earthy, and sensory. Focuses on the elements: wind, dust, the weight of denim, the scent of cedarwood, leather, and rain. Avoids abstract or illogical comparisons (e.g., \"her soul was a storm\").\nPacing Signature: Slow-burn tension that builds to explosive, high-heat payoffs. Scenes linger on quiet moments of proximity (in a barn, on a porch, in a truck cabin) to build anticipation, ending chapters on emotional cliffhangers or unresolved physical tension.\nSentence Structure: Traditional compound and complex structures that establish a steady, comforting rhythm. Avoids the \"rule of three\" for list-making adjectives or verbs."

Use a beat of silence or a physical action instead.\nDo not use abstract, poetic nonsense to describe attraction (e.g., \"their souls danced in a cosmic alignment\"). Keep it physical and raw.\nDo not group things in threes. Avoid lists of three adjectives, three verbs, or three short sentences in a row.\nDo not use interpretive commentary to explain a character's action (e.g., \"He looked away, showing his guilt\"). Let the action speak for itself.\nDo not write characters who apologize constantly or over-communicate their feelings early on. The tension relies on their silence and emotional armor.\nDo not use pointless bookend summary sentences at the end of a scene or chapter.\n\nVoice Markers\nShowing Emotion Physically\nInstead of: I was incredibly nervous and felt so small standing in front of him.\nWrite: My throat went dry as I stepped closer, my boots sinking into the gravel. I had to tilt my chin up just to meet his eyes.\nGrounded, Concrete Imagery\nInstead of: He smelled like a rugged fantasy of pine and dark secrets.\nWrite: He smelled of cedarwood, horse sweat, and the sharp, clean scent of the rain that had just started to hit the dirt.\nAvoiding the Rule of Three\nInstead of: He stood up, walked to the window, and stared out at the rain.\nWrite: He stood up and walked to the window, his eyes locking on the dark clouds rolling over the ridge.\nEliminating Interpretive Commentary\nInstead of: Maeve slid the plate toward me in a motherly gesture of peace.\nWrite: Maeve slid the warm plate of peach pie across the laminate counter, her eyes holding mine until I took the fork.\n\nBANNED WORDS (DO NOT USE THESE WORDS/PHRASES):\nAVOID these overused AI words and phrases: \"tapestry\", \"testament\", \"symphony\", \"labyrinth\", \"shivers down spine\", \"let out a breath they didn't know they were holding\", \"eyes flashed\", \"needless to say\", \"in a world where\", \"a dance of\", \"delve\".\n\nANTI-PATTERNS (AVOID THESE STRUCTURES):\nAVOID structural AI patterns:\n- Do not end chapters with moralizing summaries, rhetorical questions, or neat wrap-ups.\n- Avoid overly balanced dialogue where everyone speaks in complete, polite paragraphs.\n- Avoid sudden, unearned emotional shifts or overly therapeutic language (\"I see you\", \"your feelings are valid\").\n\n---------------------------------------\n\nWrite Chapter 1: \"The Lantern at the Gate\".\n\nChapter Summary: Wren Delaney drives her battered Subaru into Mariposa, Texas, the late-afternoon sun turning the scrubland gold and merciless. She is humming to herself—a habit she has never fully controlled—and her floral sundress and turquoise Etsy boots are a deliberate act of will against the gray exhaustion she is carrying underneath. She stops first at Delaney Veterinary Clinic, where her father's name is still stenciled on the frosted glass door in faded gold letters, and the living quarters behind the office turn out to be a disaster of collapsed shelving and a slow roof leak her father forgot to mention. Her professional armor holds, but just barely. Then Maeve Holloway appears, as if summoned, with an offer already decided: the Barn Apartment on the Lantern Ranch, clean and ready and only forty yards from the main house. Maeve serves Wren a slice of apple pie at the clinic's break table while the paperwork is settled, and the warmth of it—the deliberate, unhurried kindness—nearly cracks Wren open. Driving through the Lantern Ranch gate at dusk, Wren notices the unlit carriage lantern hanging dark at the post, its glass clouded with years of dust, and something about it lodges in her chest like a splinter she can't locate.\n\nOverall Novel Synopsis: In the sun-scorched heart of West Texas, Wyatt Holloway has spent ten years building a prison out of duty. When his father Hank died in a ranch fire hours after their worst fight—the morning Wyatt told him he'd never want to be like him—Wyatt swallowed his dream of veterinary school whole and became Hank instead, rough edges and all, as if sheer penance could settle a debt with a dead man. He runs the Lantern Ranch with his jaw set and his heart locked, and the unlit carriage lantern hanging at the gate suits him fine. He doesn't deserve the light. Then Dr. Wren Delaney comes home to Mariposa, Texas, floral sundresses and granola bars and all, to take over her father's veterinary clinic—and Maeve Holloway, the family's quietly omniscient matriarch, rents her the Barn Apartment on the Lantern Ranch before Wyatt can form a single objection. Suddenly the one woman who represents every dream Wyatt buried is living forty yards from his bedroom window, humming in the morning dark and smelling like coffee and wildflowers, and the armor he's spent a decade welding shut starts to crack at every seam.\n\nWren Delaney knows exactly what she looks like to men like Wyatt: naive, soft, too much color for a serious world. She has let people believe that her relentless brightness is simply who she is, because the truth—that she built her sunshine like a fortress, brick by deliberate brick, after a brutal Dallas emergency shift gutted her professional confidence and left her quietly falling apart—is hers to carry alone. Coming home to Mariposa isn't retreat; it's reclamation. She intends to earn her name on that clinic door and prove to every stubborn rancher in the county that she is exactly as capable as her father. What she doesn't expect is Wyatt—surly and immovable and heartbreaking in his self-imposed exile—or the way her memory of the boy he used to be keeps surfacing when she watches his calloused hands turn impossibly gentle during a difficult calving, revealing the gifted, wasted talent underneath all that deliberate hardness. She refuses to fix him. She refuses to dim herself for him. But she cannot stop herself from wanting him to choose to come back to life.\n\nTheir proximity does its slow, inevitable work: livestock emergencies that require trust, Sunday dinners at Maeve's table where the brothers orbit Wren like she's always belonged, and late-night conversations on the Barn Apartment steps where ten years of silence begin to fracture. When Wyatt finally tells Wren about the fight—the exact words he said to his father the morning of the fire—it costs him everything he has left to give, and it is the first honest thing he has offered the world in a decade. Their hard-won beginning is earned not through grand gestures but through the small, daily rebellions of a man learning to want his own life again and a woman who has always known that joy is not a gift but a choice. The night they finally find their footing, headlights sweep slow across the Lantern Ranch drive. An engine cuts. A car door opens in the dark. Caleb Holloway, absent for years and carrying something the family doesn't yet understand, has come home—and the questions surrounding the fire that broke this family are only beginning to surface.\n\nEnsure the chapter is well-written, engaging, and flows naturally. Do not include any out-of-character AI pleasantries. Begin the text immediately."

Hard Rules (Always Do)\n\n- **Show, Don't Tell:** Convey emotion strictly through visceral physical reactions (tightening stomachs, clicking teeth, white knuckles).\n- **Ground the Scene:** Anchor the characters in the physical reality of the small town (weather, textures, specific physical geography).\n- **Drive Banter:** Allow dialogue to overlap, clash, and spark without characters over-explaining what they mean.\n\n### Hard Avoids (Never Do)\n\n- **Never Name Emotions:** Do not write \"She was furious\" or \"He felt sad.\"\n- **No Interpretive Gestures:** Never explain an action (e.g., \"She folded her arms in a defensive gesture\").\n- **No \"Rule of Three\":** Ban 3-part lists (\"smart, capable, and determined\" or \"stopped, stared, and listened\").\n- **No Sensory/Emotion Combos:** Never pair a scent with a feeling (e.g., \"pine and longing\").\n- **No Formulaic Phrasing:** Ban \"It's not x, it's y or z,\" \"It feels x because it is x,\" and \"X matters.\"\n- **No Rhetorical Q&A:** Ban internal monologue asking a question and immediately answering it.\n- **No Redundant Adjectives or Illogical Metaphors:** Ban \"loud and strident,\" \"sandpaper on silk,\" \"symphony of...\", \"tapestry of...\"\n- **No Dialogue Echoes:** Characters must never repeat the other's statement back to them as a question.\n- **No Bookends:** Ban trailing, philosophical summary sentences at the end of a scene (\"He wondered what the future held\").\n\n### Voice Markers (Example Phrases)\n\n- *Instead of naming emotion:* \"I gripped the door handle, knuckles turning white against the hot metal.\"\n- *Instead of three-part staccato:* \"He just hooked his thumbs into the front pockets of his jeans, looking entirely too comfortable in the brutal afternoon sun.\"\n- *Instead of interpretive dialogue tags:* \"'I'll walk into town,' I told him, turning toward the county road.\"\n### Sample Prose\n\nThe check engine light had been mocking me for thirty miles, but it finally made good on its threat right in front of the local feed store. Gray smoke billowed from the hood of my sedan, smelling strictly of burning rubber and melted plastic. I grabbed my purse and kicked the driver’s door open. Heat rolled off the asphalt in suffocating waves, instantly sticking my cotton shirt to my back. Of all the places to break down, it had to be his driveway. Heavy boots crunched against the gravel, stopping just short of my bumper. I didn't need to look up past the faded denim and silver belt buckle to know who they belonged to.\n\n\"You always did know how to make an entrance.\"\n\nThe gravelly drawl hit me right in the chest. I gripped the door handle, knuckles turning white against the hot metal. I pushed myself out of the driver's seat and shoved the door shut. The hinge let out a screech that echoed across the empty dirt lot.\n\n\"I was aiming for a quiet homecoming,\" I said, swatting a horsefly away from my cheek. \"The radiator had a different agenda.\"\n\nHayes didn't laugh. He just hooked his thumbs into the front pockets of his jeans, looking entirely too comfortable in the brutal afternoon sun. \"Quiet isn't exactly your specialty.\"\n\nHe walked past me to the front of the sedan. He didn't ask permission before reaching under the latch and popping the hood. A fresh cloud of acrid smoke puffed into the air. He coughed once, waving a calloused hand through the gray haze.\n\n\"You're going to need a tow.\" He wiped a smear of black grease directly onto his thigh. \"And probably a mechanic who performs miracles.\"\n\nMy stomach tightened. I didn't have the money for a tow, let alone a miracle. I crossed my arms, keeping my focus on the hissing engine rather than the broad span of his shoulders. Ten years hadn't softened the rigid lines of his posture. If anything, the Texas sun had baked him into something harder.\n\n\"I'll walk into town,\" I told him, turning toward the county road. \"I can call someone from the diner.\"\n\n\"The diner closed an hour ago.\" He slammed the hood down. The metal-on-metal crash made my teeth snap together. \"And town is five miles away.\"\n\nI stopped walking. \"I have good shoes on.\"\n\nHe dropped his gaze to my frayed canvas sneakers, then brought it slowly back up to my face. The corner of his mouth twitched. \"Get in the truck, Harper.\"\n\n\"I'd rather walk.\"\n\n\"You'll get heatstroke before you hit the city limits.\" He pulled a set of keys from his pocket and unlocked a dented black Silverado parked by the barn. \"Get in the truck.\"\n\nBANNED WORDS (DO NOT USE THESE WORDS/PHRASES):\nAVOID these overused AI words and phrases: \"tapestry\", \"testament\", \"symphony\", \"labyrinth\", \"shivers down spine\", \"let out a breath they didn't know they were holding\", \"eyes flashed\", \"needless to say\", \"in a world where\", \"a dance of\", \"delve\".\n\nANTI-PATTERNS (AVOID THESE STRUCTURES):\nAVOID structural AI patterns:\n- Do not end chapters with moralizing summaries, rhetorical questions, or neat wrap-ups.\n- Avoid overly balanced dialogue where everyone speaks in complete, polite paragraphs.\n- Avoid sudden, unearned emotional shifts or overly therapeutic language (\"I see you\", \"your feelings are valid\").\n\n---------------------------------------\n\nWrite Chapter 1: \"The Lantern at the Gate\".\n\nChapter Summary: Wren Delaney drives her battered Subaru into Mariposa, Texas, the late-afternoon sun turning the scrubland gold and merciless. She is humming to herself—a habit she has never fully controlled—and her floral sundress and turquoise Etsy boots are a deliberate act of will against the gray exhaustion she is carrying underneath. She stops first at Delaney Veterinary Clinic, where her father's name is still stenciled on the frosted glass door in faded gold letters, and the living quarters behind the office turn out to be a disaster of collapsed shelving and a slow roof leak her father forgot to mention. Her professional armor holds, but just barely. Then Maeve Holloway appears, as if summoned, with an offer already decided: the Barn Apartment on the Lantern Ranch, clean and ready and only forty yards from the main house. Maeve serves Wren a slice of apple pie at the clinic's break table while the paperwork is settled, and the warmth of it—the deliberate, unhurried kindness—nearly cracks Wren open. Driving through the Lantern Ranch gate at dusk, Wren notices the unlit carriage lantern hanging dark at the post, its glass clouded with years of dust, and something about it lodges in her chest like a splinter she can't locate.\n\nOverall Novel Synopsis: In the sun-scorched heart of West Texas, Wyatt Holloway has spent ten years building a prison out of duty. When his father Hank died in a ranch fire hours after their worst fight—the morning Wyatt told him he'd never want to be like him—Wyatt swallowed his dream of veterinary school whole and became Hank instead, rough edges and all, as if sheer penance could settle a debt with a dead man. He runs the Lantern Ranch with his jaw set and his heart locked, and the unlit carriage lantern hanging at the gate suits him fine. He doesn't deserve the light. Then Dr. Wren Delaney comes home to Mariposa, Texas, floral sundresses and granola bars and all, to take over her father's veterinary clinic—and Maeve Holloway, the family's quietly omniscient matriarch, rents her the Barn Apartment on the Lantern Ranch before Wyatt can form a single objection. Suddenly the one woman who represents every dream Wyatt buried is living forty yards from his bedroom window, humming in the morning dark and smelling like coffee and wildflowers, and the armor he's spent a decade welding shut starts to crack at every seam.\n\nWren Delaney knows exactly what she looks like to men like Wyatt: naive, soft, too much color for a serious world. She has let people believe that her relentless brightness is simply who she is, because the truth—that she built her sunshine like a fortress, brick by deliberate brick, after a brutal Dallas emergency shift gutted her professional confidence and left her quietly falling apart—is hers to carry alone. Coming home to Mariposa isn't retreat; it's reclamation. She intends to earn her name on that clinic door and prove to every stubborn rancher in the county that she is exactly as capable as her father. What she doesn't expect is Wyatt—surly and immovable and heartbreaking in his self-imposed exile—or the way her memory of the boy he used to be keeps surfacing when she watches his calloused hands turn impossibly gentle during a difficult calving, revealing the gifted, wasted talent underneath all that deliberate hardness. She refuses to fix him. She refuses to dim herself for him. But she cannot stop herself from wanting him to choose to come back to life.\n\nTheir proximity does its slow, inevitable work: livestock emergencies that require trust, Sunday dinners at Maeve's table where the brothers orbit Wren like she's always belonged, and late-night conversations on the Barn Apartment steps where ten years of silence begin to fracture. When Wyatt finally tells Wren about the fight—the exact words he said to his father the morning of the fire—it costs him everything he has left to give, and it is the first honest thing he has offered the world in a decade. Their hard-won beginning is earned not through grand gestures but through the small, daily rebellions of a man learning to want his own life again and a woman who has always known that joy is not a gift but a choice. The night they finally find their footing, headlights sweep slow across the Lantern Ranch drive. An engine cuts. A car door opens in the dark. Caleb Holloway, absent for years and carrying something the family doesn't yet understand, has come home—and the questions surrounding the fire that broke this family are only beginning to surface.\n\nEnsure the chapter is well-written, engaging, and flows naturally. Do not include any out-of-character AI pleasantries. Begin the text immediately."

SERIES PREMISE & LOGLINE\nLogline: Five brothers. One ranch. One lantern that hasn't been lit in ten years.\nOne-Paragraph Pitch: In the dusty West Texas town of Hart's Ridge, the five Holloway brothers have been circling the same ten-year-old grief since the night a fire took their father, their town's legendary rodeo, and the lives they thought they were going to live. Now, one summer at a time, each brother will be cracked open by a woman who sees through his particular armor — and piece by piece, the truth of what really happened that night will come home. Steamy, small-town, trope-heavy contemporary romance for readers of Elsie Silver, Lyla Sage, and Tessa Bailey.\nSeries Tagline (Marketing): Back before the stars.\nAlternate Taglines:\nSome fires don't go out.
